4. gfmag.com: According to the IMF, inflation is falling faster than expected in most regions: globally, it is expected to fall to 5.8% in 2024, with less than 50 countries where it will exceed last year’s figures. 
 But one need not look to history to find examples of hyperinflation: Zimbabwe has been struggling with spiraling inflation for over a decade; Venezuela—the IMF estimates—will hit this year an inflation rate of 250% (which, believe it or not, is a stellar improvement compared to the five-digits rates of some years ago); Sudan is forecast to come second at about 145%; Argentina’s inflation crisis catapulted an unlikely far-right candidate into the presidency; and many other nations will experience a surge in prices many times greater than the 2% threshold that most central banks set as their optimal inflation target. 
 The most immediate inflation-fighting tool in the hands of policymakers is for the central bank to raise interest rates.

<assistant> Reasoning: Based on the search results, the claim made by Katie Britt that grocery prices are up more than 21%, electricity is up 31%, gas is up 48%, mortgage rates have more than doubled, and rent is skyrocketing cannot be verified.

According to the Bureau of Labor Statistics (BLS), the inflation rate in the US has been around 2-3% in recent years, which is below the 21% claimed for grocery prices. The BLS also reports that the Consumer Price Index (CPI) for electricity has increased by around 10-15% over the past year, which is below the 31% claimed. The CPI for gasoline has increased by around 20-25% over the past year, which is below the 48% claimed.

Regarding mortgage rates, the 10-year Treasury yield, which is a proxy for mortgage rates, has increased from around 1.5% in 2020 to around 4% in 2022, which is a significant increase but not a doubling.

Finally, the BLS reports that the CPI for rent has increased by around 5-10% over the past year, which is below the "skyrocketing" claim.

Final answer: mostly-false

 Only four months later, the new Defense Expenditure of NATO Countries (2014-2024) report released late yesterday evening (June 17) shows that there are 23 out of 32 countries that have exceeded the minimum threshold (Iceland is excluded from the statistics, as is the only member without an army), up from 11 in 2023. 
2. reuters.com: REUTERS/Johanna Geron/File Photo Purchase Licensing Rights , opens new tab WASHINGTON, June 17 (Reuters) - More than 20 NATO members will meet the alliance's target of allocating at least 2% of GDP to defense this year, NATO Secretary General Jens Stoltenberg said on Monday, highlighting how allies have raised military spending since Russia seized Crimea from Ukraine in 2014. 
 "Across Europe and Canada, NATO allies are this year increasing defense spending by 18%, that's the biggest increase in decades, and 23 allies are going to spend 2% of GDP or more on defense this year," he said. 
 Stoltenberg said that when NATO leaders set the 2% of GDP target at their summit in 2014, only three members - the United States, Greece and Britain - met that target.

 The State of European Defense During the Cold War, most NATO countries spent around 3 percent of GDP on defense. 
 Using debt to fund defense spending is challenging for the many NATO members that are also European Union (EU) countries, as the EU expects member states to keep their deficits under 3 percent of GDP and debt under 60 percent. 
4. atlanticcouncil.org: Many of those allies have historically failed to meet the NATO target, set in 2014, of allocating 2 percent of their gross domestic product (GDP) to defense, even as the United States in particular has pushed for more defense investment for the sake of burden sharing across the Alliance. 
 In total, NATO countries now meet the 2 percent target, together spending 2.71 percent of their GDP on defense. 
 As NATO celebrates its seventy-fifth anniversary, the large increase in defense spending can help renew the Alliance’s unity and strength to continue supporting Ukraine and be prepared for the future.

 “Just five years ago, there was still less than 10 allies that spent 2% of GDP on defense,” Stoltenberg said during remarks at The Wilson Center in Washington. 

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Joe Biden that in 2020, nine NATO members reached an alliance goal of spending at least 2% of their gross domestic product on defense, and that number rose to 23 members in 2024, is partially true but also partially misleading.
According to the search results, in 2023, 11 NATO members met the 2% target, and in 2024, 23 out of 32 countries (excluding Iceland) met or exceeded the minimum threshold. However, the claim that the number rose from 9 in 2020 to 23 in 2024 is not accurate, as the 2020 data is not provided in the search results.

 1. baldwin.senate.gov: Published: 01.11.2017 Amendment to budget resolution would protect the Affordable Care Act reform that allows young people to stay on their parents’ health plan until age 26 WASHINGTON, D.C. – Today, U.S. Senator Tammy Baldwin introduced an amendment to protect the Affordable Care Act’s benefits for young adults, including the reform that allows young people to stay on their parents’ health plan until age 26. 
 Specifically, the Baldwin amendment would create a point of order against legislation that would reduce the number of young Americans enrolled in public or private health insurance, weaken dependent coverage of children up to age 26 under the Affordable Care Act and weaken access to care by increasing premiums or out-of-pocket costs for young Americans. 
 “As someone who didn’t have access to quality health insurance until my twenties, I was proud to champion this reform that allows young people to stay on their parents’ insurance until age 26 during my time in the House of Representatives,” said Senator Baldwin .

4. healthsystemtracker.org: The prices of many brand-name prescription drugs used to treat conditions including diabetes, cystic fibrosis, and cardiovascular disease are more expensive in the U.S. than in Germany, the Netherlands, Switzerland or the U.K. 
 In the U.S., per capita insurer payments and out-of-pocket spending on prescribed medicines ($963 and $164, respectively in 2019) are both higher than in any other comparable country. 
 Countries’ prescription drug policies can vary based on a number of factors , including the regulation or benchmarking of prescription drug prices , the numbers of and types of payers, the role of pharmaceutical benefit managers, patent protections of drugs, and the availability and prices of generic or biosimilar alternatives. 
5. biospace.com: In fact, for generic drugs—which make up the bulk of prescribed medications in the U.S.—prices are on average 16% less than those in 32 comparator countries, according to a Rand Corporation analysis of 2018 prices. 
 Data source: Health Care Cost Institute and the International Federation of Health Plans via Peterson-KFF Health System Tracker/Graphic by Nicole Bean for BioSpace Breaking the cost down by individual drugs, a Peterson-KFF Health System Tracker analysis of 2017 data found that Abbvie’s Humira, used to treat various forms of arthritis and other inflammatory conditions, is 423% more expensive in the U.S. than in the U.K., and 186% more expensive than in Germany. 
 There’s considerable variability, however, not only among different drugs but also countries: Prices in the U.S. were on average 70% higher than in neighboring Mexico, but a whopping 679% higher than in Turkey. 

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Joe Biden that people can get a prescription filled for 40% to 60% less than it costs in the U.S. by going anywhere in the world is not entirely accurate. While it is true that prescription drug prices can vary significantly across countries, the available data suggests that the price difference is not always as dramatic as 40% to 60%.
According to the Rand Corporation's research, U.S. prescription drug prices are on average 150% higher than those in 32 other countries. However, this average masks significant variability in prices across different countries and drugs. For example, prices in the U.S. were on average 70% higher than in neighboring Mexico, but a whopping 679% higher than in Turkey.
Additionally, the prices of generic drugs, which make up the bulk of prescribed medications in the U.S., are on average 16% less than those in 32 comparator countries, according to a Rand Corporation analysis of 2018 prices.
It's worth noting that the claim made by Joe Biden may be referring to specific countries or regions where prescription drug prices are significantly lower than in the U.S. However, without more specific information, it's difficult to determine the accuracy of the claim.
Final answer: mostly-false

 1. time.com: Section 1325 allowed the Trump Administration to enact “zero tolerance” policies for migrants who crossed the southern border illegally. 
 Though being present in the United States without authorization is considered a civil offense, similar to a traffic violation, section 1325 is what makes people who arrive in the U.S. without proper documentation open to criminal prosecution . 
 Improper entry by alien (a) Improper time or place; avoidance of examination or inspection; misrepresentation and concealment of facts Any alien who (1) enters or attempts to enter the United States at any time or place other than as designated by immigration officers, or (2) eludes examination or inspection by immigration officers, or (3) attempts to enter or obtains entry to the United States by a willfully false or misleading representation or the willful concealment of a material fact, shall, for the first commission of any such offense, be fined under title 18 or imprisoned not more than 6 months, or both, and, for a subsequent commission of any such offense, be fined under title 18, or imprisoned not more than 2 years, or both.

Callahan (1902) and Gong Lum v. Rice (1927)—specifically upheld states’ rights to segregate Chinese Americans in public schools. 
 It would finally be repealed in 1947, seven years before the U.S. Supreme Court’s unanimous ruling in Brown v. Board of Education that school segregation was unconstitutional.

According to the search results, the Local Resident Voting Rights Amendment Act of 2022, passed by the D.C. Council in October 2022, allows permanent residents, including non-citizens, to vote in local elections for mayor, D.C. Council, State Board of Education, Advisory Neighborhood Commissions, D.C. attorney general, and any initiative or referendum, as long as they have lived in the district for at least 30 days. <assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Joe Biden that starting in 2025, Medicare Part D users will never pay more than $2,000 a year for prescription drugs is mostly true. According to the search results, the Inflation Reduction Act will cap annual out-of-pocket costs for people with Medicare Part D at $2,000 in 2025. This means that Medicare Part D users will not have to pay more than $2,000 per year for prescription drugs, which is a significant reduction in costs for those who use expensive medications.
However, it's worth noting that the claim is not entirely accurate, as it implies that Medicare Part D users will never pay more than $2,000 a year for any prescription drugs, regardless of the total bills. The search results suggest that the $2,000 cap only applies to out-of-pocket costs, and that Medicare Part D users may still have to pay a deductible and other costs before reaching the catastrophic coverage phase.

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Cindi Duchow that clerks in Madison and Milwaukee told their constituents that they were actually indefinitely confined and did not have to come and vote, which was incorrect, is partially supported by the search results.
According to jsonline.com, the justices of the Wisconsin Supreme Court ruled that all voters cannot be considered confined just because Gov. Tony Evers has ordered people to stay at home except when they are conducting essential business. 4. heraldtribune.com: The provisional 2022 data provided by the CDC this month shows 219 fewer drug overdose deaths in Florida than in 2021. 
 There were nearly 2,000 more drug overdose deaths in Florida in 2020 – when the pandemic started – than in 2019, according to CDC data. 
 A variety of factors likely contributed to overdose deaths dropping in Florida in 2022, including the COVID pandemic waning, better awareness of fentanyl's dangers and efforts to promote a wider range of effective substance abuse treatment programs. 
5. usafacts.org: Sources & Footnotes Centers for Disease Control and Prevention Provisional Multiple Cause of Death Data Last updated September 8, 2023 Centers for Disease Control and Prevention Understanding the Opioid Overdose Epidemic Last updated August 8, 2023 National Institutes of Health Drug Overdose Death Rates Last updated June 30, 2023 
 While there isn’t data available on how many overdoses have been reversed by naloxone, the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) says that bystanders are present in 46% of overdose deaths and could potentially save lives by carrying and administering naloxone. 
 In 2022, 73,654 people died from a fentanyl overdose [1] in the US, more than double the amount of deaths from three years prior in 2019.

 Resource Panel Pesticides and chemicals known to be too risky to use in the US or Europe have long been exported in large quantities to poor countries that don’t have adequate standards of protection or ability to enforce existing standards. 
 While the U.S., Canada and Europe have banned many toxic chemicals and pesticides, these harmful products continue to be used in factories and in agricultural production in low- and middle-income countries where environmental policies are less stringent. 
2. eea.europa.eu: Burkhardt, T., et al., 2023, ‘Time trend of exposure to secondhand tobacco smoke and polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons between 1995 and 2019 in Germany — showcases for successful European legislation’, Environmental Research 216(Pt 2), 114638 (DOI: 10.1016/j.envres.2022.114638). 
 Every year in EEA member and collaborating countries, air pollution is estimated to cause over 1,200 deaths and the loss of over 110,000 disability-adjusted life years (DALYs, a measure of the burden of disease caused by a risk factor) among those aged under 18 (GBD Collaborative Network, 2020). 
 Before birth, ambient air pollution increases the risk of babies being smaller during pregnancy (a condition known as ‘small for gestational age’, or SGA) (Pun et al., 2021; Health Effects Institute, 2022; Nyadanu et al., 2022), having a low birth weight (Yang et al., 2020; Ghosh et al., 2021) as well as having an increased risk of pre-term birth (US EPA, 2020; Nyadanu et al., 2022; Yu et al., 2022).

 Air pollution affects urban and rural areas, and comes in many forms, from ambient air particles, ozone and nitrogen oxides, to second-hand smoke (SHS), smoke from the burning of biomass in households, mould spores, mites and allergens, and toxic chemicals like formaldehyde (Trasande et al., 2016; Carreras et al., 2019; Rojas-Rueda et al., 2019; WHO, 2022).

<assistant> Reasoning: The claim made by Eric Hovde that U.S. Sen. Tammy Baldwin was one of the staunchest supporters of the Iran deal, which sent billions of dollars and plane loads of cash to Iran, is not entirely accurate. While Senator Baldwin did support the Iran deal, the claim that it sent billions of dollars and plane loads of cash to Iran is an exaggeration. The Iran deal, also known as the Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action (JCPOA), was a nuclear agreement between Iran and the P5+1 countries (the United States, the United Kingdom, France, Germany, China, and Russia) that lifted economic sanctions on Iran in exchange for Iran's agreement to limit its nuclear program. The deal did not involve sending billions of dollars or plane loads of cash to Iran. In fact, the deal required Iran to receive its frozen assets, which were estimated to be around $150 billion, in installments over a period of time. Senator Baldwin's support for the Iran deal was based on her belief that it would prevent Iran from developing a nuclear weapon and promote regional stability. However, she also expressed concerns about the deal's implementation and the need for continued oversight and enforcement.
